[1:0:379,363] Resources  

[2:0:344,406] Natural Resources Agency programs protect and  

[2:1:344,415] restore California's diverse natural and cultural  

[2:2:344,425] resources for current and future generations,  

[3:0:344,435] including operating state parks, managing water  

[3:1:344,445] supplies, supporting forest health, encouraging  

[3:2:344,455] clean energy, and preserving fish and wildlife  

[3:3:344,465] habitats, rich farmlands and mineral resources.  

[3:4:344,475] Agency programs also protect the public through  

[3:5:344,485] construction and maintenance of levees in the  

[4:0:344,495] Central Valley and suppression of wildfires. These  

[4:1:344,505] programs not only contribute to the state's unique  

[4:2:344,514] quality of life, but also are critical to sustaining a  

[4:3:344,524] vibrant economy.

[1:0:242,39] 3100   Exposition Park  

[2:0:66,60] The Office of Exposition Park Management, the Science Center, and the California African American Museum (CAAM), are  

[2:1:66,70] located in Exposition Park, a 152-acre tract in south Los Angeles, which is owned by the state. For budget purposes, these  

[2:2:66,79] three departments are collectively known as Exposition Park.  

[3:0:66,97] The Office of Exposition Park Management provides long-term leadership in the development and implementation of park  

[3:1:66,106] usage policy and day-to-day management, operation and promotion of the park for its tenants and the public. The mission of  

[3:2:66,116] the Science Center is to stimulate curiosity and inspire science learning. The mission of CAAM is to research, collect, preserve,  

[3:3:66,126] and interpret, for public enrichment, the history, art, and culture of African Americans with an emphasis on California and the  

[3:4:66,136] western United States.  

[4:0:66,153] Because   the Science Center's   programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the   Science Center   has a capital outlay  

[4:1:66,163] program to support this need. For the specifics on the Science Center's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[5:0:66,238] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:0:66,257] 2300 - CALIFORNIA SCIENCE CENTER  

[7:0:66,277] Attracting over 2 million guests annually, the California Science Center represents one of California's premier educational and  

[7:1:66,286] family destinations. Governed by a nine-member Board of Directors appointed by the Governor, the Science Center develops  

[7:2:66,296] and features award-winning exhibits and internationally renowned education programs.  

[8:0:66,316] Hands-on educational exhibits and programs focus on science, math, technology, and conservation which explore biological  

[8:1:66,326] processes of humans, animals, plants, the Earth's ecosystems, habitats, and geophysical processes, as well as engineering,  

[8:2:66,335] communications, and transportation, on land and in space. The Science Center’s Air and Space Center, the permanent home  

[8:3:66,345] for the Space Shuttle Endeavour, will be constructed by the California Science Center Foundation. In July 2021, the Foundation  

[8:4:66,355] reached the fundraising threshold required to begin construction. The entire bond financing process was completed in October  

[8:5:66,365] 2021, and construction is expected to begin in early 2022.  

[9:0:66,384] In addition, the Science Center operates a 3D IMAX theater which features science related films and documentaries. The  

[9:1:66,394] Center for Science Learning offers professional development programs to improve math and science skills of teachers and  

[9:2:66,404] other educators. The Science Center School is a K-5 science, math and technology focused neighborhood charter school. As a  

[9:3:66,414] Title I school, it serves one of the more underserved and economically challenged communities in South Los Angeles. The  

[9:4:66,423] school's instructional programs and teacher training meet California science standards.  

[10:0:66,443] The Science Center's mission values accessibility and inclusiveness and strives to inspire interest in science among those  

[10:1:66,453] traditionally underrepresented in science, math, technology and engineering. The Science Center receives significant funding  

[10:2:66,462] support for exhibit development and capital outlay projects from the California Science Center Foundation.  

[11:0:66,480] 2305 - EXPOSITION PARK MANAGEMENT  

[12:0:66,499] The Office of Exposition Park Management (OEPM) is responsible for public safety operations, parking services management  

[12:1:66,509] and overall park beautification maintenance. In addition, OEPM administers supervision and coordination of park-wide events,  

[12:2:66,519] and facilitates communication efforts between state, city, county, and private entities represented within the park. OEPM is  

[12:3:66,529] responsible for the negotiations of ground leases and agreements which impact the state's interest, including development of  

[12:4:66,538] park facilities and future infrastructure improvements. OEPM is also responsible for managing the Exposition Park  

[13:0:66,548] Improvement Fund and generating revenue to maintain the park. Exposition Park enriches the internal park community and in  

[13:1:66,558] addition provides green space and recreational areas for the surrounding neighborhood.  

[14:0:66,575] 2310 - CALIFORNIA AFRICAN AMERICAN MUSEUM  

[15:0:66,592] The California African American Museum (CAAM) is governed by a seven-member board of directors appointed by the  

[15:1:66,602] Governor. CAAM’s mission is to research, collect, preserve, and interpret, for public enrichment, the history, art, and culture of  

[15:2:66,612] African Americans with an emphasis on California and the western United States. Founded in 1977, as the first African  

[15:3:66,622] American museum of art, history, and culture fully supported by a state, CAAM was the direct result of a sustained, multiyear  

[15:4:66,631] campaign of activism undertaken by visionary founders and community members. CAAM occupies a 44,000-square-foot facility  

[15:5:66,641] in Exposition Park, which includes 15,000 square feet of gallery space and an additional 13,000 square feet of space for public  

[15:6:66,651] programming. The museum’s permanent collection houses 4,000 objects that span paintings, photographs, film, sculpture,  

[15:7:66,661] historical documents, and artifacts. CAAM has a strong mandate to support the work of under-represented artists of color and  

[15:8:66,671] to think expansively about the way that people of the African diaspora have contributed to American culture and the world at  

[15:9:66,680] large. That is achieved through a rotating exhibition schedule and public programming for visitors of all ages. More than forty  

[15:10:66,690] years after its founding, the originating principle for CAAM remains vital to a healthy and just America--to create and support an  

[15:11:66,700] inclusive, accessible, and dynamic space where all people can see black lives, culture, and experiences valued and reflected.  

[16:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[6:1:66,231] 2320 - TAHOE REGIONAL PLANNING AGENCY

[7:1:66,260] Agency coordinates planning and regulations that preserve and enhance the environment and resources of the Lake Tahoe  

[7:2:66,270] Basin. Funding for the Agency is shared between Nevada (one-third) and California (two-thirds).

[9:2:66,373] projects under the federal Sea Grant Program.

[6:0:201,200] 3125   California Tahoe Conservancy  

[7:0:66,221] The California Tahoe Conservancy's mission is to lead California's efforts to restore and enhance the natural and recreational  

[7:1:66,231] resources of the Lake Tahoe Basin (Basin). The Conservancy achieves its mission by developing and implementing programs  

[7:2:66,240] that acquire environmentally sensitive land; provide grants for or directly fund soil erosion prevention and watershed  

[7:3:66,250] restoration; and improve stream environments, wildlife habitats, forest health, and water quality. The Conservancy balances  

[7:4:66,260] these activities with programs to improve public access and recreational opportunities in an environmentally sound manner by  

[7:5:66,270] acquiring lakefront property, constructing pedestrian and bike paths, and building public access facilities.  

[8:0:66,289] The Conservancy is also a key implementing agency of the Environmental Improvement Program (EIP), which is intended to  

[8:1:66,299] restore and protect the Basin's natural resources.   The EIP leverages funding from the states of California and Nevada, the  

[8:2:66,309] Tahoe Regional Planning Agency, the federal government, local governments, and various private entities for projects needed  

[8:3:66,319] to achieve environmental goals in the Basin. The EIP is updated periodically to include more refined estimates of projects,  

[8:4:66,328] modifications in the scope of identified projects, and inclusion of new projects. 

[9:0:66,348] Because the Conservancy's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Conservancy has a capital outlay program  

[9:1:66,358] to support this need. For the specifics on the Conservancy's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[11:0:66,539] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[11:1:66,558] 2340 - TAHOE CONSERVANCY  

[12:0:66,575] The Conservancy manages nearly 4,700 state-owned properties totaling more than 6,500 acres, including thousands of  

[12:1:66,585] quarter-acre lots within the Basin’s urban areas. These parcels provide open space, water quality and recreational benefits,  

[12:2:66,594] and have significantly reduced the potential level of development in the Basin. The Conservancy manages its lands for multiple  

[12:3:66,604] benefits and inspects each parcel once every two years to monitor forest health and to identify maintenance needs and  

[12:4:66,614] encroachments. Maintaining healthy forests in urban areas to help protect life and property constitutes another fundamental  

[12:5:66,624] role of the Conservancy’s land management program. The Conservancy also manages 11 properties with recreational  

[12:6:66,633] amenities, including 6 beaches that provide public access to Lake Tahoe.  

[13:0:66,651] The Conservancy and its partners are increasingly shifting to large landscape, multi-jurisdictional, multiple-benefit projects to  

[13:1:66,661] more rapidly and comprehensively achieve restoration goals in the Basin. The Conservancy plans to continue working with a  

[13:2:66,670] wide variety of partners to restore the health and resilience of the Basin’s forests and watersheds, with the aim of also  

[13:3:66,680] increasing the pace and scale of restoration. This includes improving forest diversity, protecting wildlife, applying beneficial fire,  

[13:4:66,690] clearing meadows of encroaching trees, reducing streambank erosion, reconnecting floodplains, and treating storm water. This  

[13:5:66,700] work will enhance the ability of these lands to respond to wildfire, drought, insects, and climate change.  

[14:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[2:0:166,32] 3125   California Tahoe Conservancy - Continued 

[3:0:66,68] The Conservancy also works closely with local governments to meet its mission by providing grants for acquisitions and site  

[3:1:66,78] improvements such as building roadside and storm drainage facilities and sediment basins, building bike trails, and creating  

[3:2:66,88] public gathering spaces and improving public access to the Lake.  

[3:3:66,105] 2345 - ACQUISITION AND SITE IMPROVEMENT PROJECTS  

[4:0:66,125] This program acquires environmentally sensitive lands and provides public access and recreation by enhancing existing  

[4:1:66,135] facilities and establishing new public access sites along the Lake and in natural areas. The program also preserves a wide  

[4:2:66,144] variety of habitats supporting endangered, threatened or rare animal and plant species, and restores streams and watersheds  

[4:3:66,154] such as marshes, meadows and riparian areas to mitigate development damage and enhance water quality.  

[5:0:66,171] In addition, the program is helping to reduce greenhouse gas emissions and achieve environmental benefits by promoting  

[5:1:66,181] urban in-fill development in the Basin. These efforts include selling, leasing, or exchanging non-environmentally sensitive  

[5:2:66,191] Conservancy lands and associated development rights to support revitalization efforts consistent with local area plans and town  

[5:3:66,201] center objectives.   Property exchanges and the proceeds from the sale or leasing of property are then used to (1) acquire other  

[5:4:66,211] environmentally sensitive lands or (2) fund conservation and other projects that support the Conservancy in fulfilling its mission.

[2:0:166,32] 3125   California Tahoe Conservancy - Continued 

[3:0:66,68] The California Tahoe Conservancy manages programs to help protect Lake Tahoe's water quality and conserve wildlife habitat,  

[3:1:66,78] watershed areas, and public access on the California side of the Lake Tahoe Basin. The Conservancy is a primary implementer  

[3:2:66,88] of the State of California's responsibilities under the Environmental Improvement Program (EIP) for the Lake Tahoe Basin - a  

[3:3:66,98] 1997 agreement between California, Nevada, the Tahoe Regional Planning Agency, the federal government, local  

[3:4:66,108] governments, and various private entities. The EIP is updated periodically to include more refined estimates of projects,  

[3:5:66,117] modifications in the scope of identified projects, and new projects.

[11:0:188,481] 3210   Environmental Protection Program  

[12:0:66,502] The California Environmental Protection Program receives revenue from the sale of personalized motor vehicle license plates  

[12:1:66,512] by the Department of Motor Vehicles. Revenues are deposited in the California Environmental License Plate Fund. Funds  

[12:2:66,522] expended pursuant to Division 13.5 of the Public Resources Code must have one or more of the following purposes:  

[12:3:65,539] • The control and abatement of air pollution, including all phases of research into the sources, dynamics and effects of  

[12:4:76,549] environmental pollutants.  

[13:0:65,565] • The acquisition, preservation, restoration, or any combination thereof, of natural areas or ecological reserves.  

[13:1:65,580] • Environmental education, including formal school programs and informal public education programs.  

[13:2:65,596] • Protection of nongame species and threatened and endangered plants and animals.  

[13:3:65,612] • Protection, enhancement, and restoration of fish and wildlife habitat and related water quality, including review of the  

[13:4:76,622] potential impact of development activities and land use changes on that habitat.  

[13:5:65,638] • The purchase, on an opportunity basis, of real property consisting of sensitive natural areas for the state park system and for  

[13:6:76,647] local and regional parks.  

[14:0:65,663] • Reduction or minimization of the effects of soil erosion and the discharge of sediment into the waters of the Lake Tahoe  

[14:1:76,673] region, including the restoration of disturbed wetlands and stream environment zones.  

[15:0:66,700] The appropriations and expenditures for programs and projects funded from the California Environmental License Plate Fund  

[15:1:66,710] are contained in the budgets of the various state boards, commissions and departments.  

[16:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[11:0:200,428] 3340   California Conservation Corps  

[12:0:66,448] The California Conservation Corps (CCC) provides young women and men the opportunity to work hard responding to fires,  

[12:1:66,458] floods and other disasters, restoring California's environment, and installing clean energy and energy conservation measures at  

[12:2:66,468] public facilities throughout the state. Through their service, the corpsmembers gain life, work, and academic skills to become  

[12:3:66,478] strong workers and citizens.  

[13:0:66,497] In addition to the CCC, there are also 14 local conservation corps located in metropolitan communities throughout the state that  

[13:1:66,507] are annually certified by the CCC, and engage young people in conservation, recycling, education, and training activities.  

[14:0:66,527] Because the   CCC's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the CCC has a capital outlay program to support this  

[14:1:66,536] need. For the specifics on the CCC's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[5:0:66,151] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:1:66,170] 2360 - TRAINING AND WORK PROGRAM  

[5:2:66,187] This Program focuses on four areas:  

[6:0:66,205] Natural Resource Work: Corpsmembers protect and enhance the state's natural resources through park development, trail  

[6:1:66,214] construction, tree planting, fire hazard reduction, watershed improvement, wildlife habitat enhancement, removal of non-native  

[6:2:66,224] vegetation, meadow restoration, energy and water auditing and retrofitting, irrigation system installation, and drought-tolerant  

[6:3:66,234] and other landscaping.  

[7:0:66,251] Disaster Response: Corpsmembers are dispatched to fires, assisting with initial attack, mop-up and logistical support; floods,  

[7:1:66,261] filling sandbags, reinforcing levees and stabilizing hillsides; earthquakes, removing hazards and staffing disaster assistance  

[7:2:66,271] centers; oil spill clean-up; snow removal; search and rescue operations; pest infestation eradication; and homeland security  

[7:3:66,281] assistance.  

[8:0:66,298] Corpsmember Education: Corpsmembers are provided opportunities to advance their academic skills while in the CCC through  

[8:1:66,308] local adult and charter schools and community colleges. 

[9:0:66,325] Corpsmember Development and Training: The CCC stresses the development of both a work and service ethic, which includes  

[9:1:66,335] teamwork, self-discipline, leadership, and giving back to California. Corpsmembers learn conservation principles and career  

[9:2:66,344] planning. The CCC also offers training in trail building, first aid, Hazardous Waste Operations and Emergency Response, and  

[9:3:66,354] firefighting certification, which can lead to internship opportunities with various employers in California.

[3:0:66,69] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[4:0:66,87] The California Conservation Corps (CCC) operates 26 facilities statewide, including 10 residential and 16 non-residential  

[4:1:66,97] facilities in both urban and rural areas. These facilities enable the CCC to achieve its mission of providing employment, training,  

[4:2:66,107] and educational opportunities to young adults. The facilities also allow the CCC to assist federal, state and local agencies, as  

[4:3:66,117] well as nonprofit entities, with the conservation of California's natural resources.  

[4:4:66,134] The CCC residential facilities house 80 to 100 corpsmembers each and operate 24 hours a day, bringing young people  

[4:5:66,144] together from across the state, and increasing capacity for emergency response and conservation projects. Non-residential  

[4:6:66,154] centers range in size from 30 to 60 corpsmembers and are located in both urban and rural communities throughout California.

[5:0:66,138] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:1:66,157] 2370 - REGULATION OF ENERGY INFRASTRUCTURE SAFETY  

[5:2:66,174] Established pursuant to Chapter 81, Statutes of 2019 (AB 111), the Office is the successor to the Wildfire Safety Division of the  

[5:3:66,184] California Public Utilities Commission as of July 1, 2021 and is authorized to:  

[5:4:65,201] • Oversee and enforce electrical corporations’ compliance with wildfire safety pursuant to Chapter 6 (commencing with Section  

[5:5:76,211] 8385) of Division 4.1.  

[6:0:65,227] • In consultation with the California Wildfire Safety Advisory Board, develop and recommend to the commission performance  

[6:1:76,237] metrics to achieve maximum feasible risk reduction to be used to develop the wildfire mitigation plan and evaluate an  

[6:2:76,247] electrical corporation’s compliance with that plan.  

[6:3:65,262] • Develop a field audit program for wildfire mitigation plan compliance by each electrical corporation.  

[6:4:65,278] • Consult with the Office of Emergency Services in the office’s management and response to utility public safety power shutoff  

[6:5:76,288] events and utility actions for compliance with public safety power shutoff program rules and regulations.  

[6:6:65,304] • Support efforts to assess and analyze fire weather data and other atmospheric conditions that could lead to catastrophic  

[6:7:76,313] wildfires and to reduce the likelihood and severity of wildfire incidents that could endanger the safety of persons, properties,  

[6:8:76,323] and the environment within the state.  

[7:0:65,339] • Retain appropriate staff that includes experts in wildfire, weather, climate change, emergency response, and other relevant  

[7:1:76,349] subject matters.  

[8:0:65,365] • Review, as necessary, in coordination with the California Wildfire Safety Advisory Board and necessary commission staff,  

[8:1:76,374] safety requirements for electrical transmission and distribution infrastructure and infrastructure and equipment attached to  

[8:2:76,384] that electrical infrastructure, and provide recommendations to the commission to address the dynamic risk of climate change  

[8:3:76,394] and to mitigate wildfire risk.  

[8:4:65,410] • Perform regulatory oversight of and support to the California Underground Facilities Safe Excavation Board.

[5:0:105,211] 3360   Energy Resources Conservation and Development Commission  

[6:0:66,232] The Energy Resources Conservation and Development Commission (Energy Commission) is responsible for ensuring a  

[6:1:66,242] reliable supply of energy to meet state needs while protecting public health, safety, and the environment. Activities include the  

[6:2:66,251] following: making public policy recommendations regarding energy; collecting targeted energy data and ensuring data is  

[6:3:66,261] managed responsibly; developing and implementing research, development, demonstration, and deployment policies and  

[6:4:66,271] programs that promote strategic energy investments; ensuring energy facilities approved by the Energy Commission are  

[6:5:66,281] designed, constructed, operated, amended, and decommissioned in a manner that protects the environment and public health  

[6:6:66,290] and safety, and in compliance with all applicable laws, ordinances, regulations, and standards; adopting building efficiency  

[6:7:66,300] standards, and adopting and enforcing appliance efficiency standards; promoting development, deployment, and integration of  

[6:8:66,310] zero-emission and near zero-emission transportation infrastructure and technology; and supporting climate change goals  

[6:9:66,320] through integrated resource planning, renewable energy development, and system integration.

[6:0:66,269] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[7:0:66,288] 2380 - REGULATORY and PLANNING PROGRAM  

[8:0:66,315] The Regulatory and Planning program facilitates markets in providing adequate statewide energy supplies. The program  

[8:1:66,324] certifies power plant sites, develops and implements landscape-scale plans to identify suitable areas for renewable energy  

[8:2:66,334] resources and infrastructure development, designates transmission line corridors, and monitors new energy facilities and fuel  

[8:3:66,344] infrastructure for compliance and supply and distribution adequacy, consistent with state energy policies. The program  

[8:4:66,354] develops state energy policy based on the economic, security, safety, and environmental land-use and climate implications of  

[8:5:66,363] energy supply and demand. The Energy Assessments program develops long-range projections for future energy supplies and  

[8:6:66,373] demand, maintains current information on statewide electrical generation sources, consumption and peak demand, natural gas,  

[8:7:66,383] transportation fuels, and distributed generation. The program develops state energy policy based on the economic, financial,  

[8:8:66,393] security, safety, and environmental implications of supply, demand, and price/cost assessments, and monitors energy markets  

[8:9:66,403] for competitive prices and potential market abuses.  

[9:0:66,420] 2380010 - Power Plant Site Certification and Transmission Line Corridor Designation Program 

[10:0:66,437] The Power Plant Site Certification and Transmission Line Corridor Designation programs are responsible for the following:  

[10:1:66,447] reviewing thermal power plant siting applications 50 megawatts and larger; ensuring power plant owners, operators, and  

[10:2:66,457] developers comply with their specific conditions of certification and all applicable laws and regulations when constructing,  

[10:3:66,466] operating, and closing power plants, and investigating potential violations; reviewing project change petitions to amend filed  

[10:4:66,476] applications; reviewing applications for transmission line corridor designation; developing environmental and land use  

[10:5:66,486] information and evaluating constraints and opportunities for renewable energy infrastructure development; and analyzing  

[10:6:66,496] safety, reliability, efficiency, resilience, and environmental performance issues related to power plants and the state electricity  

[10:7:66,506] transmission grid.  

[11:0:66,523] 2380019 - Electricity Analysis  

[12:0:66,540] The Electricity Analysis program is responsible for evaluating current and future electricity supply market trends and  

[12:1:66,550] infrastructure needs; estimating costs and emissions of various generation types; assessing the adequacy of electricity  

[12:2:66,560] supplies; and analyzing regulations and environmental issues related to electricity. Additionally, this program addresses the  

[12:3:66,569] distribution system and its relationship to distributed generation, including planning and interconnection barriers and provides  

[12:4:66,579] information and recommendations to state agencies, electricity market participants, and the public. The natural gas program  

[12:5:66,589] component is responsible for evaluating current and future natural gas market trends and infrastructure needs, assessing the  

[12:6:66,599] adequacy of natural gas supplies, monitoring the interaction of natural gas and electricity to maintain system reliability,  

[12:7:66,609] analyzing regulations and environmental issues related to natural gas, and leading interagency work groups to monitor short-

[12:8:66,618] term natural gas market trends. The program also collects, monitors, and analyzes data on transportation fuel supply and  

[12:9:66,628] production, fuel infrastructure issues, and responds to energy and fuel shortages and emergencies.  

[13:0:66,645] 2385 - ENERGY RESOURCES CONSERVATION PROGRAM  

[14:0:66,672] The Energy Resources Conservation program objectives include reducing overall energy use and decreasing peak electricity  

[14:1:66,682] demand by identifying energy efficiency opportunities and developing and implementing programs and standards for efficiently  

[14:2:66,692] using energy in all sectors.  

[15:0:66,709] 2385010 - Building and Appliances Program  

[16:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

# === PAGE 35 === [size: 612x792]
[0:0:66,13] NATURAL RESOURCES 

[1:0:412,13] 2023-24 STATE BUDGET — RES   33 

[2:0:70,32] 3360   Energy Resources Conservation and Development Commission - Continued 

[3:0:66,68] The Building and Appliances program objectives include reducing consumer and business energy use, water use, and peak  

[3:1:66,78] demand by developing and implementing strategies and minimum efficiency and flexible demand standards for appliances,  

[3:2:66,88] equipment, and new and existing residential and nonresidential buildings. The Building and Appliances program provides  

[3:3:66,98] technical assistance on building standards and enforcement support to the building industry and building departments. Further,  

[3:4:66,108] the program maintains a database of efficient appliances and equipment and enforces compliance with the appliance efficiency  

[3:5:66,117] standards.  

[4:0:66,135] 2385019 - Energy Projects Evaluation and Assistance Program  

[5:0:66,152] This program is responsible for providing technical assistance, grants, and loans to improve energy efficiency and reduce  

[5:1:66,162] operating costs of schools, colleges, local jurisdictions, hospitals, and public care facilities.  

[6:0:66,179] 2385028 - Demand Analysis  

[7:0:66,196] The Demand Analysis program is responsible for collecting and analyzing electricity and natural gas consumption data used to  

[7:1:66,206] prepare energy demand forecasts, forecasting peak and total energy consumption by sector, and estimating the amount of  

[7:2:66,216] energy conserved by existing and proposed program activities. The program conducts detailed electricity and natural gas end-

[7:3:66,226] use surveys which serve as a primary data source for energy demand forecasts, conservation potential studies, and market  

[7:4:66,235] and policy impact assessments. The program also performs transportation energy demand forecasting, conducts consumer  

[7:5:66,245] vehicle choice surveys, and assesses the impacts of transportation electrification on California electricity use.  

[8:0:66,262] 2390 - DEVELOPMENT PROGRAM  

[9:0:66,289] The Development Program focuses on transportation and alternatives to conventional fossil fuels. The mission is to support  

[9:1:66,299] provision of adequate and reliable transportation energy to the California transportation sector while balancing economic, public  

[9:2:66,309] health, safety, and environmental consequences. The program also conducts research, development, demonstration,  

[9:3:66,319] commercialization, and deployment activities on energy efficiency, renewable and advanced energy technologies, and  

[9:4:66,329] alternative and renewable fuel and advanced vehicle technologies so that future energy supplies are: cost effective, secure,  

[9:5:66,338] and reliable; enhance environmental quality; and promote state and local economic development. The program provides  

[9:6:66,348] technical assistance, financial assistance, direct technology research and demonstration, technology forecasting, technology  

[9:7:66,358] analysis and evaluation, and knowledge sharing.  

[10:0:66,375] 2390010 - Transportation Technology and Fuels  

[11:0:66,392] The Clean Transportation Program provides financial incentives to develop and deploy innovative technologies, infrastructure,  

[11:1:66,402] and strategies that transform California's fuel and vehicle types to help attain the state's climate change and public health  

[11:2:66,412] policies. The program goal is the development, deployment and integration of zero and near-zero emission fuels and advanced  

[11:3:66,422] vehicle technologies and infrastructure. The program provides analyses and recommendations to guide state energy policy and  

[11:4:66,431] legislation to achieve climate change and air quality objectives, reduce petroleum consumption, and promote economic  

[11:5:66,441] development. The program also provides incentives to workforce development, training, and manufacturing that supports the  

[11:6:66,451] program goals.  

[12:0:66,468] 2390019 - Research and Development  

[13:0:66,486] The Research and Development program spurs innovation in energy efficiency, energy generation, storage, infrastructure  

[13:1:66,495] resiliency, renewable integration, and transportation to meet the state’s clean energy and climate goals. The programs bring  

[13:2:66,505] science and technology breakthroughs from the lab to the market to benefit all Californians, with a focus on ensuring the  

[13:3:66,515] benefits also accrue to under-resourced communities. The programs catalyze a world-class innovation ecosystem that provides  

[13:4:66,525] the resources, expertise, and information to foster and support successful clean energy entrepreneurship across the state. The  

[13:5:66,534] programs equip communities, businesses, and public agencies with breakthrough technology solutions and tools to build a  

[13:6:66,544] safe, resilient, affordable and clean energy system. The programs are administered through strategic performance-based  

[13:7:66,554] competitive solicitations.  

[14:0:66,571] 2390028 - Renewable Energy  

[15:0:66,589] The Renewable Energy program fosters growth of the renewable energy market by providing financial incentives to support the  

[15:1:66,598] development and use of eligible renewable energy products; requiring utilities to disclose information to consumers on their  

[15:2:66,608] power fuel mix; implementing the Renewables Portfolio Standard; and establishing solar equipment rating standards and  

[15:3:66,618] maintaining solar equipment lists meeting the performance and safety standards.  

[16:0:66,635] 2395 - LOAN REPAYMENT PROGRAM  

[17:0:66,662] The Loan Repayment program consists of Conservation and Development program loan repayments deposited into the  

[17:1:66,672] following accounts: State Energy Conservation Assistance Account; Energy Technologies Research, Development and  

[17:2:66,682] Demonstration Account; and Local Government Geothermal Resources Revolving Subaccount.  

[18:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[8:0:66,390] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[9:0:66,409] 2420 - GEOLOGIC HAZARDS AND MINERAL RESOURCES CONSERVATION  

[10:0:66,426] This program evaluates, assesses and maps the state's geologic and seismologic hazards, such as earthquakes, landslides,  

[10:1:66,436] tsunami and volcanic eruption threats, and hazardous minerals exposures, in order to protect the public health and safety and  

[10:2:66,446] the natural environment; analyzes the state's mineral assets; and maps its mineral resources. Information is used by federal,  

[10:3:66,456] state, and local government agencies, industries and individual businesses, and the public to make informed decisions about  

[10:4:66,465] land use, seismic safety, and mineral development.  

[11:0:66,483] 2425 - GEOLOGIC ENERGY MANAGEMENT DIVISION  

[12:0:66,500] This program regulates the drilling, operation, and abandonment of oil, natural gas, and geothermal wells to prevent, as   much  

[12:1:66,510] as possible, damage to life, health, property, and natural resources. The program seeks to protect public health and safety and  

[12:2:66,519] environmental quality, including reduction and mitigation of greenhouse gas emissions associated with the development of  

[12:3:66,529] hydrocarbon and geothermal resources in a manner that meets the energy needs of the state. The state is fully reimbursed for  

[12:4:66,539] program expenditures by annual assessments and fees on the respective industries. 

[13:0:66,556] 2430 - LAND RESOURCE PROTECTION  

[14:0:66,574] This program protects agricultural farmland and open space through various financial incentives. The Williamson Act program  

[14:1:66,583] provides advice on and reviews documents related to changes to Williamson Act contracts between landowners and local  

[14:2:66,593] governments. The California Farmland Conservancy Program provides grants to local governments and nonprofit land trusts  

[14:3:66,603] for planning purposes and for the acquisition of agricultural conservation easements that permanently remove development  

[14:4:66,613] rights, and therefore development pressure, from agricultural lands. The Farmland Mapping and Monitoring Program develops  

[14:5:66,622] maps, statistics, and reports relating to farmland conversion, farmland inventory, and land protection to assist in local land use  

[14:6:66,632] decisions.  

[15:0:66,649] 2435 - MINE RECLAMATION  

[16:0:66,667] This program regulates surface mining operations and monitors local lead agencies to ensure compliance with the Surface  

[16:1:66,677] Mining and Reclamation Act of 1975. It assists cities, counties, state agencies, and mine operators in their efforts to reclaim  

[16:2:66,686] mined lands to beneficial uses. This program also compiles an inventory of the state's estimated 47,000 abandoned mines and  

[16:3:66,696] remediates abandoned mine hazards to protect public safety.  

[17:0:66,713] 2440 - STATE MINING AND GEOLOGY BOARD  

[18:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[2:0:171,32] 3480   Department of Conservation - Continued 

[3:0:66,68] The Board serves as a regulatory and policy body for the state's geology, geologic and seismologic hazards, conservation of  

[3:1:66,78] mineral resources, and reclamation of mined lands. The department's California Geological Survey and the Division of Mine  

[3:2:66,88] Reclamation provide the Board with relevant engineering, technical expertise, and support functions for certain reports, plans,  

[3:3:66,98] and maps. The Board also serves as an appeals body for mining operations that have been issued notice of violation orders to  

[3:4:66,108] comply, or administrative penalties and in cases where the Division of Mine Reclamation contests the adequacy of a local  

[3:5:66,117] government’s approval of a mine operation’s financial assurance cost estimate.

[5:0:164,168] 3540   Department of Forestry and Fire Protection  

[6:0:66,189] The Department of Forestry and Fire Protection (CAL FIRE) provides all hazard—fire, medical, rescue, and  

[7:0:66,199] disaster—emergency response to the public and provides leadership in the protection of life, property, and natural resources in  

[7:1:66,209] California.  

[8:0:66,228] CAL FIRE safeguards the public through: engineering; research, development and adoption of regulations; fire and life safety  

[8:1:66,238] programs; fire prevention, law enforcement, and public information and education; resource protection; and emergency  

[8:2:66,248] response.  

[9:0:66,267] CAL FIRE limits the damage caused by fires, disasters, environmental degradation, and related emergencies by employing  

[9:1:66,277] diverse yet complementary efforts, including: training California's fire service professionals, public education and prevention  

[9:2:66,287] awareness, responsible stewardship of our natural resources, and natural resource and emergency management.  

[10:0:66,306] CAL FIRE cultivates mutually beneficial and cost-effective relationships with all levels of government and the private sector to  

[10:1:66,316] enhance the efficiency and effectiveness of public safety programs and mitigate emergencies in the urban, rural, and wildland  

[10:2:66,326] areas of California.  

[11:0:66,345] Because CAL FIRE's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, CAL FIRE has a capital outlay program to support  

[11:1:66,355] this need. For the specifics on the CAL FIRE's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[5:0:66,151] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:0:66,170] 2461 - STATE FIRE MARSHAL  

[7:0:66,187] The Office of the State Fire Marshal protects life and property through the development and application of fire prevention,  

[7:1:66,197] engineering, education, enforcement, and regulations.  

[8:0:66,214] 2461010 - Office of the State Fire Marshal:  

[9:0:66,232] The Office of the State Fire Marshal (OSFM) is comprised of the following six divisions:  

[10:0:65,249] • Code Development and Analysis: The Code Development and Analysis division is responsible for the development and  

[10:1:76,259] adoption of codes relating to fire and life safety used statewide by architects, engineers, design professionals, and the local  

[10:2:76,268] fire and building authorities. This division fosters, promotes, and develops ways and means of protecting life and property  

[10:3:76,278] against fire in many ways, including the adoption and implementation of regulations for statewide application. This division  

[10:4:76,288] prepares the OSFM fire and life safety regulations and building standards for review and adoption into the California Code of  

[10:5:76,298] Regulations, titles 19 and 24.   The division assists local fire departments, fire districts, and building departments with the  

[10:6:76,308] application of state laws and regulations.   This division also reviews all of California's regulations relating to fire and life  

[10:7:76,317] safety for relevancy, necessity, conflict, duplication, and overlap.  

[11:0:65,333] • Fire and Life Safety: The Fire and Life Safety division is responsible for applying laws and regulations related to fire  

[12:0:76,343] prevention and life safety in state-owned and state-occupied facilities. This is achieved through code compliance inspections  

[12:1:76,353] of new and existing buildings and plan review and construction inspections. The OSFM is responsible for fire and life safety  

[12:2:76,362] in over 45,000 state-owned and state-occupied facilities, which include: state prisons, conservation camps, courts, state  

[12:3:76,372] mental hospitals, state developmental centers, California State University and University of California campuses, and  

[12:4:76,382] California Agricultural Districts.  

[13:0:65,398] • Fire Engineering and Investigations: The Fire Engineering and Investigations division’s primary functions include licensing,  

[13:1:76,408] product approval, fire/explosion investigations, and illegal fireworks enforcement and disposal. The division is responsible for  

[13:2:76,417] 11 statewide programs, which include the Building Materials Listing; Portable Fire Extinguishers; Flame Retardant Chemicals  

[13:3:76,427] and Fabrics; Vapor Recovery; Automatic Fire Extinguishing Systems; Fire Safe Cigarettes; Licensing Enforcement and  

[13:4:76,437] Inspection; Lab Accreditation; Motion Picture and Entertainment; Arson and Bomb Investigation; and Fireworks Disposal.  

[13:5:76,447] This division also oversees the Arson and Bomb Unit, which has the responsibility to dispose of seized illegal fireworks and  

[13:6:76,456] conduct fire/explosion investigations and licensing enforcement.  

[14:0:65,472] • Pipeline Safety: The Pipeline Safety division ensures the safe construction, operation, and maintenance of approximately  

[14:1:76,482] 5,400 miles of intrastate hazardous liquid pipelines that transport crude oil, refined products (e.g., gasoline, diesel, jet fuel,  

[14:2:76,492] etc.), and Highly Volatile Liquids through populated urban areas, ecologically sensitive areas, and other high consequence  

[14:3:76,502] areas. Pipelines that fall under the Pipeline Safety division authority are pipelines that transport hazardous liquids between  

[14:4:76,511] facilities, such as offshore platforms, production fields, refineries, storage terminals, and marine terminals. This division is  

[14:5:76,521] additionally charged with implementing investigations for failures, explosions, and fires on intrastate pipelines, and assisting  

[14:6:76,531] on investigations for interstate pipelines.   The Certified Unified Program Agencies program is responsible for ensuring the  

[14:7:76,541] implementation of the California Fire Code Hazardous Materials Management Plan/Hazardous Materials Inventory  

[14:8:76,550] Statement (HMMP/HMIS) and the Aboveground Petroleum Storage Act program elements.   The HMMP/HMIS program ties  

[14:9:76,560] in closely with the Hazardous Release Response Plan and Inventory or Hazardous Materials Business Plan program.   The  

[14:10:76,570] Firefighter Equipment and Foam program is tasked to monitor the use of Perfluoroalky and Polyfluoroalkyl substances  

[14:11:76,580] (PFAS) throughout the state at designated facilities, issue waivers to refineries and terminals for fixed suppression systems  

[14:12:76,589] meeting identified criteria.  

[15:0:65,605] • State Fire Training: The State Fire Training division administers the California Fire Service Training and Education System  

[15:1:76,615] and the Fire Service Training and Education Program for federal, state, and local firefighters. The division oversees a  

[15:2:76,625] California Fire Academy System for over 65 training academies that represent a partnership with the fire departments,  

[15:3:76,635] community colleges, and the OSFM. State Fire Training administers a professional certification system for fire service  

[15:4:76,644] personnel and receives program guidance from the State Board of Fire Services.  

[16:0:65,660] • Wildfire Planning and Engineering: The Wildfire Planning and Engineering division will be transitioning into the new  

[17:0:76,670] Community Wildfire Preparedness and Mitigation Division established by the passage of Chapter 225, Statutes of 2021 (AB  

[17:1:76,680] 9). The division is responsible for various community-level fire prevention and preparedness programs including defensible  

[17:2:76,689] space, home hardening, fire planning, land use planning, utility fire mitigation, fire prevention grants, fire hazard mapping,  

[17:3:76,699] incident data and statistics, and damage inspection.  

[18:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[2:0:129,32] 3540   Department of Forestry and Fire Protection - Continued 

[3:0:66,68] 2461019 - California Underground Facilities Safe Excavation Board:  

[4:0:66,86] Also called the “Dig Safe Board,” created by the passage of Chapter 809, Statutes of 2016 (SB 661), the Board works on  

[4:1:66,96] issues related to the state's "Call Before You Dig" law, which requires excavators and buried infrastructure owners to coordinate  

[4:2:66,105] to prevent infrastructure damage and unsafe outcomes.   The Dig Safe Board is charged with coordinating the state’s safe  

[4:3:66,115] excavation outreach and education efforts, developing safe excavation standards, investigating accidents and working with  

[4:4:66,125] partner state agencies to enforce the “Call Before You Dig” law.   The Dig Safe Board was transferred to the Office of Energy  

[4:5:66,135] Infrastructure Safety on January 1, 2022 to comply with the requirements of Chapter 307, Statutes of 2020 (SB 865).  

[5:0:66,152] 2465 - FIRE PROTECTION  

[6:0:66,169] CAL FIRE provides fire protection to keep damages to life, property, and natural resources within social, political, and economic  

[6:1:66,179] constraints.   The objective is to attack fires quickly and aggressively in areas where CAL FIRE has assumed primary direct  

[6:2:66,189] protection responsibility by virtue of law, contract, or agreement until the fire is under control.   The level of initial attack and  

[6:3:66,198] follow-up action is relative to values threatened and control difficulty with the goal to control all unwanted fires within the first  

[6:4:66,208] burning period.  

[7:0:66,226] 2465010 - Fire Prevention:  

[8:0:66,243] This program is responsible for implementation of the Strategic Fire Plan, fire engineering, law enforcement, fire suppression  

[8:1:66,253] cost recovery, and fire prevention education.   This program focuses on the most effective methods, materials, and procedures  

[8:2:66,262] to mitigate hazard and to enforce pertinent laws that deter and mitigate fires.   More specifically, efforts focus on what needs to  

[8:3:66,272] be done before a wildland fire starts to reduce the costs of firefighting, property loss, injury to the public and fire fighters, and  

[8:4:66,282] damage to the environment.  

[9:0:66,299] 2465019 - Fire Control:  

[10:0:66,316] The objective of this program is to detect, respond to, and suppress wildland fires in or threatening the State Responsibility  

[10:1:66,326] Area.   The heart of the effort is an aggressive initial attack strategy.   CAL FIRE's goal is to contain 95 percent of all wildfires to  

[10:2:66,336] 10 acres or less.   This is achieved through detection, ground attack, air attack, and mutual aid using fire engines, fire crews,  

[10:3:66,346] bulldozers, helicopters, and fixed wing aircraft.  

[11:0:66,363] 2465028 - Cooperative Fire Protection:  

[12:0:66,380] CAL FIRE provides a full range of fire protection services to local, county, state, and federal agencies throughout California  

[12:1:66,390] through the administration of over 100 cooperative fire protection service agreements.   Additionally, there are six long standing  

[12:2:66,400] agreements wherein counties provide wildland fire protection on behalf of the Department.  

[13:0:66,417] 2465037 - Conservation Camps:  

[14:0:66,434] CAL FIRE, in cooperation with the Department of Corrections and Rehabilitation, Divisions of Adult Parole Operations and  

[14:1:66,444] Division of Juvenile Justice, operates 30 conservation camps throughout the state with capacity to house 152 fire crews.   CAL  

[14:2:66,454] FIRE operates seven permanent Fire Centers, six seasonal Fire Centers, and one Parole Training Center with the California  

[14:3:66,464] Conservation Corps throughout the state that house 28 fire crews.   CAL FIRE also operates in conjunction with the California  

[14:4:66,474] National Guard to staff seven Fire Centers with a total of 10 fire crews.   Additionally, CAL FIRE is currently authorized to  

[14:5:66,483] operate 16 CAL FIRE Firefighter Crews located at seven different Fire Centers.   These crews are available to respond to all  

[14:6:66,493] types of emergencies including wildfires, floods, search and rescue, and earthquakes.   When not responding to emergencies,  

[14:7:66,503] the crews engage in hazardous fuels reduction, conservation related work projects, and prescribed fire project work to protect  

[14:8:66,513] the community and resources of California.  

[15:0:66,530] 2465046 - Emergency Fire Suppression:  

[16:0:66,547] CAL FIRE incurs additional emergency fire suppression expenditures when budgeted initial attack forces are unable to cope  

[16:1:66,557] with a wildland fire within the initial attack period.   Portions of these costs may be reimbursed in the fiscal year the expense was  

[16:2:66,567] incurred or in a future fiscal year via Fire Management Assistance Grants provided by the Federal Emergency Management  

[16:3:66,576] Agency or through cost shares provided by other government entities.   In addition, CAL FIRE may recover portions of this cost  

[16:4:66,586] through civil cost recovery actions authorized by state statutes.  

[17:0:66,604] 2470 - RESOURCE MANAGEMENT  

[18:0:66,621] California's state and private forest, range, watershed lands, and urban forests provide multiple human, climate, and  

[18:1:66,631] environmental benefits.   The objective of this program is to maintain and enhance those benefits and to minimize damage to  

[18:2:66,640] these resources from natural catastrophes and human development.   Objectives are met by regulation of timber harvesting,  

[18:3:66,650] coordination of climate and forest restoration related activities for the forest sector, technical assistance to non-industrial  

[18:4:66,660] landowners, operation of state demonstration forests, operation of forest nurseries, vegetation management projects, and  

[18:5:66,670] administration of federal forestry assistance programs.  

[19:0:66,687] 2470010 - Resources Protection and Improvement:  

[20:0:66,704] The objective of this program is to improve forest lands and urban forests.   Activities include: (1) forest restoration through fuels  

[20:1:66,714] reduction and fire reintroduction; (2) the detection, evaluation and control of forest pests; (3) growing and selling tree seedlings  

[21:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:66,68] for reforestation, carbon sequestration through reforestation, biomass utilization, and wood products development; (4) avoided

[3:2:66,88] bank; (7) advice and assistance to non-industrial forest landowners on management of forests and improved harvesting and  

[3:3:66,98] conservation practices; (8) research on and demonstration of sustainable forestry in state forests; (9) implementation of the  

[3:4:66,108] California Forest Improvement Act of 1978; (10) technical assistance and grants to foster the environmental, social, and  

[3:5:66,117] economic benefits of urban forests consistent with the California Urban Forestry Act of 1978; and (11) purchase and monitoring  

[3:6:66,127] of working forest conservation easements per the California Forest Legacy Act of 2008.   In the area of vegetation management,  

[3:7:66,137] assistance is provided to public and private landowners to achieve land use objectives by reducing damage from wildland fires,  

[3:8:66,147] increase wildlife habitat, increase productivity of forest and rangelands, improve water yields and air quality, reduce carbon  

[3:9:66,156] emissions from wildland fires, increase firefighter safety, and maintain desirable ecosystems. 

[4:0:66,174] CAL   FIRE cooperates with federal, state and local agencies, local organizations, and private property owners to develop and  

[4:1:66,183] achieve land use objectives.   Activities include removal, rearrangement, conversion, or improvement of vegetation using  

[4:2:66,193] various treatment measures such as prescribed fire and mechanical, manual, and biological methods.   The Environmental  

[4:3:66,203] Protection Program provides in-house expertise and oversight of the Department's compliance with the California  

[5:0:66,213] Environmental Quality Act, the National Environmental Policy Act, and other environmental laws and regulations applicable to  

[5:1:66,223] departmental actions.   The Climate and Energy program represents the Department in Natural and Working Lands and Climate  

[5:2:66,232] Change Scoping Plan planning.  

[6:0:66,250] 2470019 - Forest Practice Regulations:  

[7:0:66,267] The objective of this program is to maximize sustained production of high-quality timber products while protecting soil, water,  

[7:1:66,277] wildlife, recreation, and other values associated with forest land.   Forest Practice activities include the review and permitting of  

[7:2:66,286] commercial timber harvesting operations on nonfederal timberlands, enforcing state forest practice laws and regulations, and  

[7:3:66,296] monitoring compliance and the effects of timber operations on forest productivity, water quality, watersheds, soils, botanical  

[7:4:66,306] resources, wildlife, fisheries, greenhouse gases, and other environmentally sensitive resources.   The program is also  

[7:5:66,316] responsible for issuing licenses to timber operators (LTOs), providing LTO training and education, and assisting the Board of  

[7:6:66,326] Equalization-Timber Tax Division in their administration of taxes on timber and timberland.   Forest Practice program staff also  

[7:7:66,335] assist the Board of Forestry and Fire Protection, and facilitate and participate in monitoring the effectiveness of the Board's  

[7:8:66,345] forest practice regulations.  

[8:0:66,362] 2470028 - Forest Resource Inventory and Assessment:  

[9:0:66,380] The objective of this program is to perform a periodic assessment of California's forest and rangeland resources.   Activities  

[9:1:66,389] include maintaining and distributing mission-critical datasets for fire planning, emergency incident support, and meeting certain  

[9:2:66,399] regulatory requirements.   In addition, the program provides extensive technical and public information for statewide fire threat,  

[9:3:66,409] fire hazard, watersheds, socio-economic conditions, environmental indicators, and urban forestry.  

[10:0:66,426] 2470037 - Professional Foresters Registration Program:  

[11:0:66,444] The Office of Professional Foresters Registration is a statutorily authorized program within the Board of Forestry and Fire  

[11:1:66,453] Protection.   The program is responsible for the licensing and regulation of all persons who practice forestry on non-federal,  

[11:2:66,463] state, and private lands in California pursuant to the Professional Foresters Law of 1972.   The program also licenses “certified  

[11:3:66,473] specialties” of forestry, with the single certified specialty currently being Certified Rangeland Managers.  

[12:0:66,490] 2475 - BOARD OF FORESTRY AND FIRE PROTECTION  

[13:0:66,507] The Board of Forestry and Fire Protection is a Governor-appointed body within CAL FIRE. The Board is responsible for  

[13:1:66,517] developing the general forest policy of the state, determining the guidance policies of CAL FIRE, and representing the state's  

[13:2:66,527] interest in federal forests in California. Together, the Board and CAL FIRE work to carry out statutory mandates to protect and  

[13:3:66,537] enhance the state's unique forest and wildland resources. The Board's statutory responsibilities are to:  

[14:0:65,554] • Establish and administer forest and rangeland policy for the State of California;  

[15:0:65,570] • Protect and represent the state's interest in all forestry and rangeland matters;  

[16:0:65,586] • Provide direction and guidance to the Director of CAL FIRE on fire protection and resource management;  

[17:0:65,601] • Deliver a comprehensive regulatory program for forestry and fire protection; and  

[18:0:65,617] • Conduct its duties to inform and respond to the people of California.  

[19:0:66,634] 2480 - DEPARTMENT OF JUSTICE LEGAL SERVICES  

[20:0:66,652] The objective of the Department of Justice Legal Services program is to account for the cost of legal services provided by the  

[20:1:66,661] Department of Justice to CAL FIRE.

[22:2:66,716] executive office and through central services in accounting, budgeting, business services, legal, human resources, information

[8:0:66,452] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[9:0:66,471] The Department of Forestry and Fire Protection (CAL FIRE) operates more than 596 facilities statewide. These facilities  

[9:1:66,480] include, but are not limited to, 236 fire stations, 13 air attack bases, 10 helitack bases, 30 conservation camps, 39 fire centers  

[9:2:66,490] (16 CAL FIRE, 15 California Conservation Corps, and 8 California Military Department), 112 telecommunications facilities, 16  

[9:3:66,500] administrative headquarters, 21 unit headquarters, 14 state forests, 1 forestry nursery, 1 statewide seedbank, 3 training  

[9:4:66,510] centers, and over 100 other miscellaneous facilities supporting the Fire Protection, Resource Management, and Office of the  

[9:5:66,519] State Fire Marshal. CAL FIRE facilities support fire protection and resource   management efforts for more than 31 million acres  

[9:6:66,529] of both state and privately-owned wildlands throughout California.

[0:0:66,13] 2023-24 STATE BUDGET — RES   92   NATURAL RESOURCES 

[1:0:215,89] 3560   State Lands Commission  

[2:0:66,110] The California State Lands Commission provides effective stewardship of the lands, waterways, and resources entrusted to its  

[2:1:66,120] care through preservation, restoration, enhancement, responsible economic development, and the promotion of public access.  

[2:2:66,130] Execution of the Commission's responsibilities since its inception in 1938 has resulted in the generation of billions of dollars   in  

[2:3:66,139] revenues, while protecting and enhancing the public's ability to enjoy these state lands and resources.  

[3:0:66,157] Consisting of the Lieutenant Governor, the State Controller, and the Governor's Director of Finance, the California State Lands  

[3:1:66,166] Commission serves as a trustee for the people of the state, managing approximately 4 million acres of California's sovereign  

[3:2:66,176] public trust lands and resources, received upon admission into the Union in 1850. It also manages other lands that the federal  

[3:3:66,186] government conveyed to the state (commonly known as school lands) and oversees the management of public trust lands  

[3:4:66,196] legislatively granted in trust to over 70 local jurisdictions. These trust grants encourage responsible development,  

[4:0:66,205] environmental preservation and enhancement, and public access on the state's tidelands, consistent with the common law  

[4:1:66,215] Public Trust Doctrine. The grants typically require grantees to reinvest revenues generated from the granted lands back into the  

[4:2:66,225] trust to the benefit of all Californians.  

[5:0:66,242] Public trust, or sovereign lands, include the beds of natural and navigable waterways, including non-tidal rivers, streams and  

[5:1:66,252] lakes, and tide and submerged lands within tidal rivers, sloughs, bays, and the Pacific Ocean extending from the ordinary high  

[5:2:66,262] water mark seaward to the three-mile offshore limit. In addition to sovereign lands, the Commission manages school lands,  

[5:3:66,272] which were granted to California by the federal government in 1853 for the purpose of supporting public education in California.  

[5:4:66,281] School lands typically include the 16th and 36th sections of each township and lands known to be mineral in character. Of the  

[5:5:66,291] five and one-half million acres of school lands originally granted to the state, only about 458,843 acres remain in state  

[6:0:66,301] ownership, and these are mostly concentrated in the California desert. The Commission also retains a reserved mineral interest  

[6:1:66,311] in approximately 790,000 acres of sold school lands.  

[7:0:66,328] Because the State Lands Commission’s (SLC) programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, SLC has a capital outlay  

[7:1:66,338] program to support this need. For the specifics on the SLC's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[10:0:66,487] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[11:0:66,505] 2560 - MINERAL RESOURCES MANAGEMENT DIVISION  

[12:0:66,523] The   Mineral Resources Management Division   oversees the efficient and safe development of mineral resources on state lands.  

[12:1:66,532] The state also has a beneficial interest in the Long Beach tidelands oil fields, and as such, the Commission participates in the  

[12:2:66,542] safety and fiscal oversight of these fields. The program objectives are to: manage the responsible extraction of oil, gas,  

[12:3:66,552] geothermal resources and other solid minerals; collect royalty and other revenues generated therefrom; and ensure that public  

[12:4:66,562] safety and environmental protection are maintained at the highest possible standards in the development of these resources  

[12:5:66,572] through engineering review and safety audits. The Commission also implements various programs for the prevention of oil  

[12:6:66,581] spills and the safe operation of offshore facilities and   conducts program and financial audits to ensure that the state receives  

[12:7:66,591] fair value for the development of its resources. The Commission also manages the safe and effective decommissioning of the  

[12:8:66,601] oil and gas facilities under its jurisdiction.  

[13:0:66,618] Further, the Commission manages the mineral resources associated with school lands under its purview, including revenue-

[13:1:66,628] generating extraction and prospecting leases and other projects. The Commission also coordinates with other agencies to  

[13:2:66,638] administer the statewide Abandoned Mines Program.  

[13:3:66,655] 2565 - LAND MANAGEMENT DIVISION  

[14:0:66,672] The Land Management Division manages approximately 4 million acres of tide and submerged lands and the beds of natural  

[14:1:66,682] and navigable rivers, streams, lakes, bays, estuaries, inlets, and straits. These lands, often referred to as sovereign or Public  

[14:2:66,692] Trust lands, stretch from the Klamath River and Goose Lake in the north to the Tijuana Estuary in the south, and from   Lake  

[14:3:66,702] Tahoe and   the Colorado River in the east, to three miles offshore from the Pacific Coast   in the west, and include California’s  

[14:4:66,711] two longest rivers, the Sacramento and San Joaquin.  

[15:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[0:0:66,13] 2023-24 STATE BUDGET — RES   94 

[1:0:454,13] NATURAL RESOURCES 

[2:0:180,32] 3560   State Lands Commission - Continued 

[3:0:66,68] The Commission also monitors sovereign land granted in trust by the California Legislature to approximately 70 local  

[3:1:66,78] jurisdictions that generally consist of prime waterfront lands and coastal waters, including the state’s five major ports. The  

[3:2:66,88] Commission protects and enhances these lands and natural resources by issuing leases for use or development, preserving  

[3:3:66,98] and expanding public access, and resolving boundaries between public and private lands.  

[4:0:66,115] Finally, the Commission manages the state’s school lands, which seeks to develop and consolidate the state’s school-land  

[4:1:66,125] holdings to the economic benefit of the California State Teachers’ Retirement System.  

[4:2:66,142] 2570 - MARINE ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION DIVISION  

[5:0:66,159] The   Marine Environmental Protection Division   regulates marine oil terminals within California, providing the best achievable  

[5:1:66,169] protection of the public health and safety and the environment. The Division monitors the transfer of oil at marine terminals,  

[5:2:66,179] inspects all marine facilities, and reviews and approves all marine oil terminal operations manuals and training and certification  

[5:3:66,189] programs. The Division also administers Chapter 31F of the California Building Code to ensure that marine oil terminals are fit  

[5:4:66,198] for their purpose, and implements the state's Marine Invasive Species Program to prevent the introduction of nonindigenous  

[5:5:66,208] species into California waters through the operations of oceangoing vessels. The program funds and conducts scientific  

[5:6:66,218] research, develops rules and regulations to implement the Marine Invasive Species Act, and physically inspects oceangoing  

[5:7:66,228] vessels to assess compliance with vessel-reported nonindigenous species management actions.  

[5:8:66,245] 9900100 - EXECUTIVE AND ADMINISTRATION  

[6:0:66,262] The Executive and Administration program provides management, policy direction, and administrative support to the  

[6:1:66,272] Commission’s line programs.

[3:0:66,68] The California State Lands Commission operates five facilities statewide. Two are regional headquarters, each co-located with  

[3:1:66,78] a field office, one located in Sacramento and the other in Long Beach. The three remaining facilities are field offices only, one in  

[3:2:66,88] Northern California and two in Southern California. Of the five facilities, one is state-owned (Huntington Beach Field Office),  

[3:3:66,98] and the other four are in leased space.

[10:0:197,480] 3600   Department of Fish and Wildlife  

[11:0:66,501] The mission of the Department of Fish and Wildlife is to manage California's diverse fish, wildlife, and plant resources, and the  

[11:1:66,511] habitats upon which they depend, for their ecological value and for their use and enjoyment by the public.  

[12:0:66,530] This includes habitat protection and maintenance in a sufficient amount and quality to protect the survival of species and  

[12:1:66,540] natural communities. The Department is also responsible for the diversified use of fish and wildlife including recreational,  

[12:2:66,550] commercial, scientific, and educational uses.  

[13:0:66,569] Because the Department of Fish and Wildlife programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Department has a capital  

[13:1:66,579] outlay program to support this need. For the specifics on the Department's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure  

[13:2:66,589] Overview."

[10:0:66,586] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[10:1:66,605] 2590 - BIODIVERSITY CONSERVATION PROGRAM  

[11:0:66,624] This program encourages the preservation, conservation, maintenance, and restoration of wildlife resources, including the  

[11:1:66,634] Ecosystem Restoration Program, under the jurisdiction and influence of the state. Activities involve the conservation, protection  

[11:2:66,644] and management of fish, wildlife, native plants, and habitat to ensure maintenance of biologically sustainable populations of  

[11:3:66,654] those species.  

[11:4:66,671] 2595 - HUNTING, FISHING, AND PUBLIC USE PROGRAM  

[11:5:66,688] This program facilitates diverse and sustainable hunting, fishing (recreational and commercial), trapping, and other public uses  

[11:6:66,698] and associated economic benefits to the state by conserving and managing game species. Activities include collection and

[3:0:66,68] assessment of information on the distribution and abundance of game fish and wildlife to determine appropriate regulations  

[3:1:66,78] (bag limits, gear restrictions, etc.) and to monitor the effects of those regulations.  

[3:2:66,96] 2600 - MANAGEMENT OF DEPARTMENT LANDS AND FACILITIES PROGRAM  

[4:0:66,113] This program manages Department-owned or leased lands and facilities, including hatcheries, wildlife areas, ecological  

[4:1:66,123] reserves, fish and wildlife laboratories, and public access areas, to contribute to the conservation, protection, and management  

[4:2:66,132] of fish and wildlife.  

[4:3:66,150] 2605 - ENFORCEMENT  

[5:0:66,169] This program serves the public through law enforcement, public safety and hunter education. Law enforcement promotes  

[5:1:66,179] compliance with laws and regulations protecting fish and wildlife resources; and investigates habitat destruction, pollution  

[5:2:66,189] incidents and illegal commercialization of wildlife. Wardens also serve the public through general law enforcement, mutual aid  

[5:3:66,198] and homeland security.  

[6:0:66,216] 2610 - COMMUNICATIONS, EDUCATION, AND OUTREACH PROGRAM  

[7:0:66,235] This program provides resource conservation education and activities in the classroom and on public and private lands;  

[7:1:66,245] community and stakeholder outreach; and information and data using a variety of methods including publications,  

[7:2:66,255] presentations, web applications, and media relations.  

[7:3:66,272] 2615 - SPILL PREVENTION AND RESPONSE PROGRAM  

[8:0:66,292] This program minimizes damage and environmental impacts to, restores, and rehabilitates California's fish and wildlife  

[8:1:66,301] populations and their habitats from the harmful effects of oil and other deleterious material spills in marine waters and inland  

[8:2:66,311] habitats.  

[9:0:66,329] 2620 - FISH AND GAME COMMISSION  

[10:0:66,346] The California Fish and Game Commission ensures the long term sustainability of California's fish and wildlife resources by  

[10:1:66,356] guiding the ongoing scientific evaluation and assessment of California's fish and wildlife resources; setting California's fish and  

[10:2:66,365] wildlife resource management policies and ensuring these are implemented by the Department of Fish and Wildlife;  

[10:3:66,375] establishing appropriate fish and wildlife resource management rules and regulations; and building active fish and wildlife  

[10:4:66,385] resource management partnerships with individual landowners, the public and interest groups, and federal, state and local  

[10:5:66,395] resource management agencies.

[9:0:66,468] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[10:0:66,486] The Department of Fish and Wildlife manages more than 740 properties statewide, comprising more than 1 million acres  

[10:1:66,496] (approximately 679,000 acres of state-owned land and 484,000 acres owned by other entities, but managed by the  

[10:2:66,506] Department. Several state agencies purchase land for the purpose of habitat or wildlife protection, and management  

[10:3:66,516] responsibilities for these properties are often transferred to the Department; therefore, the amount of land under Department  

[10:4:66,525] control continues to increase. Examples of the properties managed by the Department include, and are not limited to: 111  

[10:5:66,535] wildlife areas, 136 ecological reserves, 138 public access areas, and 21 fish hatcheries.  

[11:0:207,572] 3640   Wildlife Conservation Board  

[12:0:66,593] The Wildlife Conservation Board protects, restores and enhances California's natural resources for wildlife and for the public's  

[12:1:66,602] use and enjoyment in partnership with conservation groups, government agencies and the people of California.

[8:0:66,310] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[8:1:66,329] 2710 - WILDLIFE CONSERVATION BOARD  

[9:0:66,346] The Wildlife Conservation Board's three main functions are: (1) land acquisition; (2) habitat protection, restoration, and  

[9:1:66,356] enhancement, including conservation of forests, wetlands, riparian habitat, oak woodlands and protection of rangeland, grazing  

[9:2:66,365] land and grasslands to support biodiversity and climate change adaptation; and (3) development of wildlife-oriented public  

[9:3:66,375] access and recreational areas.

[8:0:198,348] 3720   California Coastal Commission  

[9:0:66,368] The California Coastal Commission, comprised of 12 voting members appointed by the Governor, the Senate Rules  

[9:1:66,378] Committee, and the Speaker of the Assembly, was created by voter initiative in 1972 and was made permanent by the  

[9:2:66,388] California Coastal Act of 1976 (Coastal Act). The Coastal Act calls for the protection and enhancement of public access and  

[9:3:66,398] recreation, marine resources, environmentally sensitive habitat areas, marine water quality, agriculture, and scenic resources;  

[9:4:66,408] and makes provisions for coastal-dependent industrial and energy development. The Commission is also designated the  

[9:5:66,417] principal state coastal management agency for the purpose of administering the federal Coastal Zone Management Act in  

[9:6:66,427] California and has exclusive regulatory authority over federal activities such as permits, leases, federal development projects,  

[9:7:66,437] and other federal actions that could affect coastal zone resources and that would not otherwise be subject to state control.

[8:0:66,431] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[8:1:66,450] 2730 - COASTAL MANAGEMENT PROGRAM  

[9:0:66,470] The objectives of the Coastal Management Program are to implement coastal resources conservation through planning and  

[9:1:66,480] regulation. Activities include:  

[10:0:65,497] • Reviewing and approval of local coastal programs (LCPs), port master plans, university long-range development plans, and  

[10:1:76,507] any amendments to such plans, for consistency with the Coastal Act.  

[11:0:65,522] • Reviewing coastal development permit applications for new development in areas without a certified LCP, areas of  

[11:1:76,532] permanently retained jurisdiction (e.g., tidelands, submerged lands, and public trust lands) and limited categories of local  

[11:2:76,542] coastal development permit actions that can be appealed to the Commission.  

[11:3:65,558] • Monitoring and enforcement of coastal development permits.  

[11:4:65,573] • Reviewing federal activities for consistency with the Coastal Act.  

[11:5:65,589] • Protecting and expanding opportunities for public coastal access and recreation.  

[11:6:65,605] • Implementing a coastal water quality protection program.  

[12:0:65,621] • Providing technical information and assistance to support effective coastal management.  

[12:1:65,637] • Implementing a coastal and ocean resource public education program.

[7:0:210,285] 3760   State Coastal Conservancy  

[8:0:66,306] The State Coastal Conservancy develops and implements projects to protect, restore, and enhance natural, recreational, and  

[8:1:66,316] economic resources along California's coast, coastal watersheds, the ocean, and within the San Francisco Bay Area. The  

[8:2:66,325] Coastal Conservancy supports implementation of the San Francisco Bay Area Conservancy Program and the Santa Ana River  

[8:3:66,335] Conservancy Program. Through technical assistance, direct funding, and grants to local agencies and nonprofit organizations,  

[8:4:66,345] the Coastal Conservancy supports projects that:  

[9:0:65,362] • Protect and enhance the natural environment, agricultural lands, scenic lands, and urban waterfronts along the Coast and  

[9:1:76,372] around San Francisco Bay.  

[9:2:65,388] • Increase public access to the coast and San Francisco Bay.  

[9:3:65,403] • Build trails and increase outdoor recreational opportunities.  

[9:4:65,419] • Enhance coastal wetlands and other important habitat areas.  

[10:0:65,435] • Prepare for and reduce the unavoidable impacts of climate change on the coastal region and San Francisco Bay Area.  

[10:1:65,451] • Support education projects and programs related to the preservation, enhancement, and enjoyment of coastal resources.  

[10:2:65,467] • Facilitate improvement of existing, and the development of, new, lower cost accommodations within one and one-half miles  

[10:3:76,476] of the coast.  

[11:0:66,506] 3-YEAR EXPENDITURES AND POSITIONS
